[12] Chapter 1: The Dawn
[13] 1
[14] The next day, we went to the Inari Shrine.
[15] Boring scenery.
[16] The asphalt road, with no branching off, twisted and turned like a snake that had fainted from pain. The forests on both sides, with their subtle elegance, reminded one of Dutch landscape paintings. The setting sun gently swayed the contours of the leaves, casting its light on the car's hood.
[17] "It's like a landlocked island," Mutsuji's exaggeration was not entirely unfounded, as the scenery outside the window had remained unchanged for the past few minutes, like a frozen frame from a video.
[18] The lush green oaks that must have been beautiful and verdant in the summer turned dull and dreary as soon as winter arrived, with only shades of dark gray and deep brown in sight, further emphasizing the monotony of the scenery.
[19] "Mom used to tell me that people shouldn't waste time," Mutsuji suppressed a yawn and murmured from the passenger seat.
[20] Since earlier, the car radio had been broadcasting a ten-kilometer traffic jam on the Osaka Expressway. Traveling through this desolate area, the normally helpful traffic information now sounded like nothing more than an irritating noise.
[21] "Although it's boring to be stuck in traffic, at least there's a clear object to vent anger against, which is quite good."
[22] "Is that what your mother said?"
[23] "Yes," Muto answered listlessly.
[24] My mother never taught me that, so I couldn't ask any further. More importantly, saying something meaningless when there's no meaning doesn't cancel anything out. Muto also remained silent, switching the radio channels.
[25] At the northern end of the Kyoto Basin lies a place called "Kama," and further north is Mount Danwa, which directly pierces the Sea of Japan. The residence of the Miura family is located there. The natural scenery, untouched by the hustle and bustle of the times, spreads out in all directions, with countless sights to behold. If one were to seek to retire and become a hermit, this place would be the most suitable.
[26] However, from a geographical perspective, it is only a one and a half hour drive from the city, and there is not much difference from the suburban new residential areas. Some company employees now spend two or three hours commuting, so this place might be a decent location. The environment is also favored by nature, and the land prices are particularly cheap.
[27] After a while, we arrived at a bright place. The previously unruly branches were neatly trimmed, indicating that we had entered the territory of the Matsuzaki family.
[28] There was a building resembling a door in front of us. The door was in a half-collapsed state, with a Kama-style iron gun tip pointing upwards, forming an ellipse, a rare structure in Japan. The paint had peeled off, and it seemed that the owner had no intention of repairing it.
[29] After passing through the door, we walked along a 500-meter-long tree-lined avenue. The trees, which are often seen in French films with only their tops covered in leaves, seemed to have been neglected and were half withered and weak.
[30] I slowed down in the tree-lined avenue and drove slowly.
[31] "That's it," said Kumagai, pointing with his hand.
[32] The sandy road ended at a small arc at the fountain in the distance, and our destination, the Inkyou Castle, loomed in front of us.
[33] The Inkyou Castle had a dark overall tone, resembling a giant old man squatting there.
[34] The Gothic-style mansion was built very solidly. The doorway on the side of the rough brick wall formed a semicircle, and the windows on the front were covered by thick iron shutters, with a pattern engraved on the protruding wall in front, which was worn out and difficult to see. It was probably a coat of arms or something similar.
[35] The blue roof sloped down from the center of the mansion, with two conical roofs of the same light blue color rising from each side, pointing upwards, forming a "mountain" shape. The three spires, symbolizing the sacred number "3", pierced the clouds and firmly rooted themselves to the ground.
[36] Although the Sparrow Castle was not as meticulously crafted as King Ludwig's Neuschwanstein Castle, it still evoked the feeling of viewing medieval castle ruins scattered along the Rhine River.
[37] "This is the Sparrow Castle," I exclaimed involuntarily. I had heard of it before, but this was the first time I had seen it. The color of the roofs also seemed to resemble a sparrow ready to take flight. The two towers at the ends were like its flapping wings. Existing in a place just an hour and a half from the ancient capital of Kyoto, such a building... I stood there, feeling the cultural shock, for a long time.
[38] "It's quite romantic," Mutsuji said, uninterested.
[39] "You mean 'low-level interest', right?"
[40] "It's said that the name 'Sparrow Castle' comes from the Italian poet Luigi's seventeenth-century prose poem 'The Night of the Sparrow'. In the poem, the sparrow represents the personification of death, coming to take the souls of children at dawn."
[41] "Children?"
[42] " 'At the dawn of the raven's cry, on the south wind, the death messenger arrives; at the dawn of the sleeping village, on the south wind, he appears to seek the child. His scythe... ' That's the gist of it," Mutsuji stopped reciting, and added, "However, Luigi Righetto himself was sentenced to death for murdering adult women."
[43] "How ominous," I thought, looking at Mutsuji. I wondered if it was true. A sense of unease that had been hidden before swept over me.
[44] "Oh, it's a red rose," he exclaimed, pointing out the window of the car. "Everything was prepared for this."
[45] The fountain in front of Hohenwerfen Castle imitates the Medici Garden in Italy, with water overflowing from it, calm and peaceful, perfectly blending with the surrounding atmosphere. The circular rim has several cracks, and the reddish-brown bricks add a somber mood.
[46] Loneliness and tranquility... If expressed in Japanese, my current state of mind would be like the verses left by Basho when he died.
[47] However, unlike the introspective interest of the builder, Mutsuji and I were filled with anxiety about how the future would unfold and "expectation." Such a posture, although clichéd, is not bad.
[48] Mutsuji pointed to several cars parked by the pool. The body was painted in a practical two-tone paint, completely unrelated to loneliness and quietude, and a symbol was carefully printed on the surface - Kyoto Prefectural Police. The last one was a gray box-shaped car of the forensic department.
[49] "Is something wrong?"
[50] "Of course, you should think so." Mutsuji seemed to be used to it, with a calm and composed expression.

[52]"Probably. We may have arrived a step too late. Detectives are always a move behind."

He spread his hands wide, showing surprise. Detective Tsubaki was already forty, but he had a childlike face when he smiled.

Of course, when dealing with cases, he would put on a serious face, but otherwise, he was a likable neighborhood uncle figure. [87] The floor was covered with a deep red carpet that stretched across the entire room. It appeared to be a high-quality material, with a thickness measured in centimeters, and it provided a noticeable bounce when stepped on.
[88] The staircase stood in the center of the hall, with a wide entrance and a turn leading up to the second floor. Like the National Diet Building, the staircase was also covered with a red carpet. In addition to the carpet on the floor and staircase, the wallpaper and color of the furniture were all in a uniform red hue. There seemed to be subtle differences in color, perhaps intentionally, which gave the hall a feeling of twilight even though it was daytime.
[89] There were two passages on either side of the hall, likely leading to the towers. They were dark and seemed to be swallowing up the light.

[92] His face was adorned with an enigmatic smile.

However, the few statues and ornaments from prehistoric times to the 15th and 16th centuries displayed on the wall shelves still fascinated me, a layman. This place was like a museum of ancient art, and the artifacts on display were probably acquired at great cost.

Whether intentional or due to technical limitations, the slight asymmetry saved them from a purely inorganic feel.

The chest plate has a linear and rounded surface covered with lily patterns, which is the same as the left one. " The chief showed off his knowledge, "These are all works of goldsmiths. From the precision of the engraving and the state of preservation, they are likely decorative items and belong to the later style."

[362] Chapter 2: Prologue
[363] It all started yesterday.
[364] "You're just in time," said Mukai with a smile.
[365] The listener of this sentence was me, Yuki Hikari, who occasionally dropped by the office.
[366] At the same time, Kumazawa took out two envelopes. One was white, and the other was a light brown, made of paper, and was a mixed bag sold on the market without any distinctive features.
[367] I looked at these two letters, puzzled.
[368] "They were delivered this morning."
[369] Both letters had been opened with a paper knife, so Kumazawa must have already seen their contents.
[370] It seemed that the contents of the letters met his expectations.
[371] I turned the envelopes over, but there was no name of the sender written on them. The brown paper envelope also had a thin layer of white powder on its surface, which was probably meant to extract fingerprints.
[372] Both letters were addressed to Murakami Yusuke. It was not unusual for him to receive personal letters, but recently, he had not received any. This was not because Murakami was poor at socializing or because he was a stingy man who had become an eyesore to others.
[373] Murakami's father owned a detective agency called "Murakami Detective Agency" and had around ten employees. Although the agency was small and had few employees, it was elite and had a good reputation in the industry. It was now a new rising star in Kyoto and the Kansai region.
[374] Murakami Yusuke was also one of the few capable men of the "Murakami Detective Agency" in his daily life.
[375]
[376] Of course, this is only in normal times...
[377] If I say it like this, it sounds a bit like "Superman," but in a way, Kumotsu may truly be a superhero.
[378] His specialty is not brute force, but rather his ability to logically process and deduce intelligent crimes. There is no one who can match him in history...
[379] This is his trademark, a symbol of his overall personality.
[380] It is commonly referred to as a "detective."
[381] In reality, he is a very talented detective. Mochizuki, as an employee of the Agency for Faith, has a close friendship with Inspector Jinguuji of the Metropolitan Police Department, all because he has participated in and solved several cases. The details will be revealed later, but for now, it is not an exaggeration to say that they are "best friends." Despite the difference in age and their roles as detective and police officer, they have always respected each other.
[382] Furthermore, as Mochizuki solves one mystery after another with ease, his reputation begins to have an unparalleled value among some people, just like a talisman that exerts a great effect. At some point, Mochizuki's name becomes a god in places unrelated to him and is given the title of "genius." This is similar to how the leaders of new religions are elevated to the status of gods by their calculating followers.
[383] Therefore, until recently, many private cases were not sent to the Agency for Faith but were directly commissioned to him personally. For a while, he received enough commissions to fill several buckets every day.
[384] However, this is like a temporary cold. Soon after, the invitations began to decline, and now they come only a few times a week.
[385] The reason is simple. Mochizuki has been rejecting invitations and refusing every single one of them.
[386] This is a matter of principle, just like most detectives, Wooden Bridge only takes cases that interest him, even for Inspector Jia Jingdan's personal requests. However, once he takes an interest, he will take on any case, even if it's to find a lost dog. It is not surprising that we can say that a detective is not a job but a hobby.
[387] Moreover, the content of the cases is almost trivial disputes among the upper class, which is one of the reasons why Wooden Bridge is reluctant to take them.
[388] In fact, Wooden Bridge once took a case hastily, and suffered a setback.
[389] "Staying away from such places is bound to bring bad luck."
[390] From then on, this became his catchphrase.
[391] However, the content of this letter seems to suit Mochizuki's taste, which is quite rare. He wore a strange smile - even a bit flirtatious...
[392] "Take a look at the letter, Hagio-san."
[393] Mochizuki seems to want me to read the letter in his hand first.
[394] The details of how I met Mochizuki are omitted here (if you want to know, please wait for Mochizuki's autobiography, which will soon be published). In short, the newly emerged mystery novelist - me, and my ideal protagonist in the novel - Mochizuki, have had a common interest in the field of criminal reasoning since our university days. This kind of closed circle of like-minded people is often referred to as a "clique." After graduation, our relationship has been maintained because both of our professions are not bound by time.
[395] So, on this day, I came to Mochizuki's place to play - perhaps I could even get some inspiration for my writing...
[396] "Is it okay?" I asked politely.
[397] "Of course. It doesn't look like a love letter."
[398] His indifferent demeanor doesn't seem to suggest that it's a love letter.
[399] The envelope contains only a note sheet. It is of high quality, with a light yellow color and a delicate floral pattern in the corner, and a watermark of the same flower in the center.
[400] The letter is written with a fountain pen, perhaps due to the use of light ink, the overall handwriting is light. The text arranged randomly on the paper gradually sinks to the right, with spaces between the lines. The writer seems to write quickly, with many white spaces and noticeable strokes.
[401] The content was also quite brief, or rather, impolite.
[402] The letter contained only one stiffly worded sentence: "Come to my residence at 10:00 a.m. on December 2nd for consultation."
[403] Apart from the time and place, there was no other content. Not even a mention of what the consultation was about. It was almost like a draft for a military recruitment notice.
[404] "What's this? It's quite interesting... But this attitude is too high-handed."
[405] I was taken aback. I had seen many unusual and lengthy complaint letters, but this letter was one of the most peculiar ones I had ever seen.
[406] "It may seem like that at first glance without any prior knowledge. Didn't you see the name of the sender clearly?"
[407] "Did it say?"
[408] "It's at the end."
[409] Indeed, it's quite natural for the envelope of a detective agency to have no name. Because no one wants others to know they are hiring a detective.
[410] I looked at the name written at the end, which was a signature that sank to the right and downward - Tomijiro Itô.
[411] "This Ito Kagami... is that..."
[412] "Yes! That's the Ito Kagami. I can't think of any other Ito Kagami."
[413] In Kyoto, the surname Ito is well-known. Even those outside of Kyoto have heard of the Ito Group. The headquarters of the Ito Group is located in Osaka and it is a large corporation with assets worth hundreds of billions of yen.
[414] The history of the company is not well-known, but it is said that the group started with a textile company called "Ito Silk Factory" which was established in the Meiji era. After that, the company followed the trend of "Rich Country, Strong Army, Promote Industry" and developed successfully, gradually entering the mechanical manufacturing industry. Then, it grew into a large zaibatsu during the late Meiji and early Showa periods due to the expansion of military needs. Some say it was because of powerful supporters in the government, while others say it was due to close ties with right-wing groups, but the truth has yet to be determined.
[415] However, it is evident that the founder, Ito Multo, had the most significant role. It is said that he had the ability to judge the situation accurately. Whether it was venturing into arms manufacturing or deciding to merge with a bank during the Great Depression, it was all his decision. As for skillfully avoiding the GHQ's policy of breaking up zaibatsu and maintaining a stable position, it was also due to his talent.
[416] After the war, Takano died. His son, the second-generation president, Motoyuki Kinmiri, showed a talent comparable to that of Takano, and after a period of rapid growth, the current Kinmiri Heavy Industry has become a super-large corporation with dozens of subsidiaries under its belt.
[417] In addition to these well-known success stories, there is another reason why the Kinmiri family is so famous.
[418] "Going to the Crow Castle."
[419] I turned the letter over.
[420] The main interest of the public is the mansion - Crow Castle. In a corner of the ancient capital, a timeless mansion, the people living there... Furthermore, the residents of the Kinmiri family have recently begun to live a life of recluses, which has further aroused people's curiosity. The more mysterious and secretive something is, the more people are attracted to it, from this point of view, the Kinmiri family and Crow Castle are the best targets.
[421] In fact, very few people have seen the mansion. Many people don't even know its exact address. No, maybe they don't even know if it exists. People only hear legends.
[422] However, the vague feeling has fueled the spread of rumors, and when people think of Kurokawa Castle, they think of Bluebeard's villa or Dracula's castle.
[423] "I heard the funniest rumor that Kurokawa Castle is an alien base," said Mukawa.
[424] It was only recently that the name Mukayama appeared in the public eye, bringing a sense of reality. A month ago, the second-generation president and chairman of Mukayama Seisakusho, Mukayama Tadashi, passed away at the age of 95. Major newspapers naturally devoted entire pages to this sad news. The scene of the funeral attendees' cars clogging the West Road is still vivid in memory.
[425] Perhaps this letter is somehow related to Mukayama Tadashi's death. At least, Mukawa thought so.
[426] "This is a big catch..."
[427] I pretended to joke, and Mutsuwa grinned. This smile is one of his seven peculiarities.
[428] "After reading the next letter, you will be moved."
[429] Mutsuwa was referring to the envelope made of brown paper. Perhaps due to the effect of sunlight, it gave an impression of being old and worn.
[430] "Is this another 'Omura'?"
[431] Inside the envelope is a folded note that is a bit thick. The paper is as unremarkable as the envelope.
[432] As soon as I opened the note, the messy handwriting caught my eye.
[433] Death is lurking in the city of Sōyama. Stay away!
[434] The words are cut from a newspaper, but the font and size are all over the place. The glue is also not enough, and the edges are curled up, as if the maker was impatient. Some characters are even replaced with katakana. However, I feel that these inconsistencies effectively contribute to the purpose of intimidation at this moment.
[435] "A rustic method."
[436] I admitted my opinion. It's rare to find such a threatening letter nowadays. Could it be a remnant of the nostalgia trend?
[437] "After all, this threatening letter is pointless. It will only make you itch."
[438] As the saying goes, a silent pheasant doesn't get shot. This vintage letter is undoubtedly the "cluck" of the pheasant. In fact, Murakami's excitement in showing me the letter revealed his true intentions.
[439] "Indeed, it's quite interesting." Murakami looked at me and spoke in a matter-of-fact tone, "You'll naturally come with me, right?"
[440] "If possible, of course. I'd like to meet the author of this threatening letter, even if they're an old-fashioned person."
[441] This is half a joke. I agreed to be Mukai's sidekick without much thought.
[442] Who could have imagined that such a tragic incident was waiting for us?
[443] "This sender…" Mukai picked up the threatening letter and smiled, "He either knows nothing about me or knows me well."

[499] The marble door was the only entrance.
[500] "It's just talk," the detective immediately denied, as if it was his duty to do so.

[504] "Cutting off the head is easy, but a locked room is not so simple... and it's meaningless."

[506] "I don't know yet."
[507] "So, we don't know the reason for making the scene a locked room either, right? Even in a mystery novel, there are occasionally locked rooms with a certain inevitability."

[3281] "Don't be in such a hurry. The killer won't get away."
[3282] "As for the locked-room murder, the important thing here isn't 'HOW' but 'WHY.' In other words, the key isn't how the locked room was made, but why it was made a locked room.
[3283] "In novels, they usually focus on the methodology, but overlook the necessity. But if we really delve into it, the 'why' is actually more important. I've summarized the motives for forming a locked room, even though Carl has also mentioned them."
[3284] "You're not going to give us a lecture on locked-room mysteries now, are you?"
[3285] When I said this, McQuade's face clouded over for a moment.
[3286] "There's still plenty of time. Moreover, my summary is concise and won't take too long. Most importantly, we need this lecture to uncover the killer."
[3287] The last sentence was quite suspicious, but since McArthur had said so, I had to give up. He was, after all, the main character in this case.
[3288] "Listen up. There are six categories of reasons for creating a locked room. Let's try to fit this case into each one.
[3289] "The first category is to disguise it as a suicide. This is a frequent motive in classical works and the most reasonable explanation. However, in the case of Ito-Hama, Ito's head and Hama's body were found in the same locked room. The killer had set up this situation, so the suicide theory is completely ruled out.
[3290] "The second category is to point the finger at a specific person. In other words, the person who holds the key or, like in 'The Judas Window', the person who was with the victim at the time of the crime, is naturally considered the killer. However, this does not fit the case either. Because Yashiro has been killed and there is no indication that the killer intended to point the finger at the maid."
[3291] "The third category is to hinder the prosecution of the crime. No matter how strong the evidence against the killer, if the mystery of the locked room cannot be solved, it cannot be prosecuted. However, as H.M. in 'The Peacock Feather Murder Case' said, this is a very negative tactic.
[3292] "Moreover, when weighing the risks of the locked room mystery being solved, it is a very dangerous attempt. Because the locked room limits the creator of the locked room. Can a high IQ killer who can create a locked room really be satisfied with creating a locked room that can be cracked at any time and be finished? I don't think so.
[3293] "For this purpose, the locked room is usually combined with the first two categories of reasons, so it can also be ruled out. The most important thing is that killing later Aoyagi and Shizuma is enough to establish the murder charge. Therefore, creating a locked room is meaningless.
[3294] "The fourth category is purely accidental, and the killer has no intention of creating a locked room, but it happens to become a locked room, or it just happens to be a locked room. In this case, if it were a latch or a latch, it would be fine, but the fact that the keyhole had a key inserted and the key was held in the left hand of the deceased is impossible both in common sense and theory. Therefore, this category can also be ruled out.
[3295] "The fifth category is that the locked room is meaningless and has no necessity, and it is created solely to satisfy the killer's vanity. In other words, it is a game. This is the solution adopted by the Mukuzuki, which is the most likely explanation at present (because it lacks necessity). And there is no evidence to refute this idea."
